class="" style="width:100%; text-align:center; border-top:1px #aaa solid"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_program" title="Discovery program">Discovery program</a></div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">&#8592;&#160;<i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)" title="Deep Impact (spacecraft)">Deep Impact</a></i></div><div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)">Kepler</a></i>&#160;&#8594;</div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>Dawn</b></i> is a retired <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> launched by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> in September 2007 with the mission of studying two of the three known  <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">protoplanets</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180907_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180907-1">[1]</a></sup> It was retired on 1 November 2018 and it is currently in an uncontrolled orbit about its second target, the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> Ceres. <i>Dawn</i> is the first spacecraft to orbit two extraterrestrial bodies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siliconvalleyastrolecture_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-siliconvalleyastrolecture-7">[7]</a></sup> the first spacecraft to visit either Vesta or Ceres, and the first to visit a dwarf planet, arriving at Ceres in March 2015, a few months before <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> flew by <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> in July 2015.\n</p><p><i>Dawn</i> entered orbit around Vesta on July 16, 2011, and completed a 14-month survey mission before leaving for Ceres in late 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5SepDepart_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-5SepDepart-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aug262012_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aug262012-9">[9]</a></sup> It then entered orbit around Ceres on March 6, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150306_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150306-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman2015.03.06_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman2015.03.06-11">[11]</a></sup> NASA considered, but decided against, a proposal to visit a third target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge20160701_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge20160701-12">[12]</a></sup> On October 19, 2017, NASA announced that the mission would be extended until the probe\'s hydrazine fuel supply was used up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20171019_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20171019-13">[13]</a></sup> On November 1, 2018, NASA announced that the Dawn spacecraft had finally exhausted all of its hydrazine fuel, thus ending its mission. The satellite is currently in an uncontrolled state about Ceres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>Dawn</i> mission was managed by NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a>, with spacecraft components contributed by European partners from Italy, Germany, France, and the Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amspace20171008_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-amspace20171008-15">[15]</a></sup> It was the first NASA exploratory mission to use <a href="/wiki/Ion_thruster" title="Ion thruster">ion propulsion</a>, which enabled it to enter and leave the orbit of two celestial bodies. Previous multi-target missions using conventional drives, such as the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program"><i>Voyager</i> program</a>, were restricted to <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">flybys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman_2006_605&#8211;616_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman_2006_605&#8211;616-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toctext">Mission summary</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Launch_preparations"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Launch preparations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Launch"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Launch</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Transit_(Earth_to_Vesta)"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Transit (Earth to Vesta)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Vesta_approach"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Vesta approach</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Vesta_orbit"><span class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">Vesta orbit</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Transit_(Vesta_to_Ceres)"><span class="tocnumber">5.6</span> <span class="toctext">Transit (Vesta to Ceres)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Ceres_approach"><span class="tocnumber">5.7</span> <span class="toctext">Ceres approach</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Ceres_orbit"><span class="tocnumber">5.8</span> <span class="toctext">Ceres orbit</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Mission_conclusion"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Mission conclusion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Media"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Media</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#High-resolution_image"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">High-resolution image</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Ceres_atlas_images"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Ceres atlas images</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Maps_of_Ceres"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Maps of Ceres</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Flyover_videos"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Flyover videos</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_history">Project history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Project history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technological_background">Technological background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Technological background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SERT-1_spacecraft.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2360" data-file-width="2954" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/SERT-1_spacecraft.jpg/150px-SERT-1_spacecraft.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/SERT-1_spacecraft.jpg/225px-SERT-1_spacecraft.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/SERT-1_spacecraft.jpg/300px-SERT-1_spacecraft.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SERT-1_spacecraft.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>SERT-1: first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_engine" title="Ion engine">ion engine</a> NASA spacecraft;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> launched on July 20, 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cybulski_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cybulski-17">[17]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The first working ion thruster in the US was built by <a href="/wiki/Harold_R._Kaufman" title="Harold R. Kaufman">Harold R. Kaufman</a> in 1959 at NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Research_Center" title="Glenn Research Center">Glenn Research Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>. The thruster was similar to the general design of a gridded electrostatic ion thruster with <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> as its propellant. Suborbital tests of the engine followed during the 1960s, and in 1964 the engine was tested on a suborbital flight aboard the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SERT-1_(spaceship)" title="SERT-1 (spaceship)">Space Electric Rocket Test 1</a> (SERT 1). It successfully operated for the planned 31 minutes before falling back to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glenn_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn-18">[18]</a></sup> This test was followed by an orbital test, SERT-2, in 1970.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Deep_Space_1" title="Deep Space 1">Deep Space 1</a> (DS1), which NASA launched in 1998, demonstrated the long-duration use of a <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a>-propelled ion thruster on a science mission,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MD_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-MD-19">[19]</a></sup> and validated a number of technologies, including the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Solar_Technology_Application_Readiness" title="NASA Solar Technology Application Readiness">NSTAR</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic_ion_thruster" title="Electrostatic ion thruster">electrostatic ion thruster</a>, as well as performing a flyby of an asteroid and a comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawn_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn-20">[20]</a></sup> In addition to the ion thruster, among the other technologies validated by the DS1 was the <a href="/wiki/Small_Deep_Space_Transponder" title="Small Deep Space Transponder">Small Deep Space Transponder</a>, which is used on <i>Dawn</i> for long-range communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawn_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_Program_selection">Discovery Program selection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Discovery Program selection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>26 proposals were submitted to the <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Program" title="Discovery Program">Discovery Program</a> solicitation, with budget initially targeted at 300 million USD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-21">[21]</a></sup> Three semi-finalists were downselected in January 2001 for a phase-A design study: Dawn, Kepler, and INSIDE Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>   In December 2001 NASA selected the Kepler and the Dawn mission for the Discovery program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-21">[21]</a></sup> Both missions were initially selected for a launch in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancellation_and_reinstatement">Cancellation and reinstatement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Cancellation and reinstatement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The status of the <i>Dawn</i> mission changed several times. The project was cancelled in December 2003,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> and then reinstated in February 2004. In October 2005, work on <i>Dawn</i> was placed in "stand down" mode, and in January 2006, the mission was discussed in the press as "indefinitely postponed", even though NASA had made no new announcements regarding its status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> On March 2, 2006, <i>Dawn</i> was again cancelled by NASA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The spacecraft\'s manufacturer, <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Sciences_Corporation" title="Orbital Sciences Corporation">Orbital Sciences Corporation</a>, appealed NASA\'s decision, offering to build the spacecraft at cost, forgoing any profit in order to gain experience in a new market field. NASA then put the cancellation under review,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and on March 27, 2006, it was announced that the mission would not be cancelled after all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In the last week of September 2006, the <i>Dawn</i> mission\'s instrument payload integration reached full functionality. Although originally projected to cost US$373 million, cost overruns inflated the final cost of the mission to US$446 million in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christopher_T._Russell" title="Christopher T. Russell">Christopher T. Russell</a> was chosen to lead the <i>Dawn</i> mission team.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_background">Scientific background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Scientific background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_and_Vesta,_Moon_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/200px-Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/300px-Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/400px-Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_and_Vesta,_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scale comparison of Vesta, Ceres, and Earth\'s moon</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i>Dawn</i> mission was designed to study two large bodies in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> in order to answer questions about the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, as well as to test the performance of its <a href="/wiki/Ion_thruster" title="Ion thruster">ion thrusters</a> in deep space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180907_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180907-1">[1]</a></sup> Ceres and Vesta were chosen as two contrasting <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">protoplanets</a>, the first one apparently "wet" (i.e. icy and cold) and the other "dry" (i.e. rocky), whose <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> was terminated by the formation of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. The two bodies provide a bridge in scientific understanding between the formation of <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">rocky planets</a> and the icy bodies of the Solar System, and under what conditions a rocky planet can hold water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU) adopted a new <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">definition of planet</a> on August 24, 2006, which introduced the term "<a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a>" for <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">ellipsoidal</a> worlds that were too small to qualify for planetary status by <a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">"clearing their orbital neighborhood"</a> of other orbiting matter. <i>Dawn</i> is the first mission to study a dwarf planet, arriving at Ceres a few months before the arrival of the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> probe at <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> in July 2015.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19547-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-AnimationFrame25-20150504.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="665" data-file-width="774" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/PIA19547-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-AnimationFrame25-20150504.jpg/200px-PIA19547-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-AnimationFrame25-20150504.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/PIA19547-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-AnimationFrame25-20150504.jpg/300px-PIA19547-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-AnimationFrame25-20150504.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/PIA19547-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-AnimationFrame25-20150504.jpg/400px-PIA19547-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-AnimationFrame25-20150504.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19547-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-AnimationFrame25-20150504.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Dawn</i> image of Ceres from 13,600 km, May 4, 2015</div></div></div>\n<p>Ceres comprises a third of the total mass of the asteroid belt. Its spectral characteristics suggest a composition similar to that of a water-rich <a href="/wiki/Carbonaceous_chondrite" title="Carbonaceous chondrite">carbonaceous chondrite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Vesta, a smaller, water-poor <a href="/wiki/Achondrite" title="Achondrite">achondritic</a> <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> comprising a tenth of the mass of the asteroid belt, has experienced significant heating and <a href="/wiki/Igneous_differentiation" title="Igneous differentiation">differentiation</a>. It shows signs of a metallic <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a>, a Mars-like density and lunar-like basaltic flows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SolarViewsCalvin_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-SolarViewsCalvin-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Available evidence indicates that both bodies formed very early in the history of the Solar System, thereby retaining a record of events and processes from the time of the formation of the terrestrial planets. <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">Radionuclide</a> dating of pieces of meteorites thought to come from Vesta suggests that Vesta differentiated quickly, in three million years or less. Thermal evolution studies suggest that Ceres must have formed some time later, more than three million years after the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ca-Al-rich_inclusions" title="Ca-Al-rich inclusions">CAIs</a> (the oldest known objects of Solar System origin).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SolarViewsCalvin_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-SolarViewsCalvin-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Moreover, Vesta appears to be the source of many smaller objects in the Solar System. Most (but not all) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vestoid" title="Vestoid">V-type</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroid" title="Near-Earth asteroid">near-Earth asteroids</a>, and some outer main-belt asteroids, have <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">spectra</a> similar to Vesta, and are thus known as <i>vestoids</i>. Five percent of the meteoritic samples found on Earth, the <a href="/wiki/HED_meteorite" title="HED meteorite">howardite&#8211;eucrite&#8211;diogenite</a> (HED) meteorites, are thought to be the result of a collision or collisions with Vesta.\n</p><p>It is thought that Ceres may have a differentiated interior;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-33">[33]</a></sup> its oblateness appears too small for an undifferentiated body, which indicates that it consists of a rocky core overlain with an icy <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-33">[33]</a></sup> There is a large collection of potential samples from Vesta accessible to scientists, in the form of over 1,400 HED meteorites,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meteoritical_Bulletin_Database_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meteoritical_Bulletin_Database-34">[34]</a></sup> giving insight into Vesta geologic history and structure. Vesta is thought to consist of a metallic iron&#8211;nickel core, an overlying rocky <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> mantle and crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahijpal2007_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sahijpal2007-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta2010_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta2010-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r874315073">.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{display:table}.mw-parser-output //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/PIA19063-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-DawnMission-March2015.jpg/1042px-PIA19063-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-DawnMission-March2015.jpg 2x" class="mw-headline" id="Objectives">Objectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Objectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif/220px-Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif/330px-Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif/440px-Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <i> Dawn</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory from 27 September 2007 to 5 October 2018<br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; <i>Dawn</i> </span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Royalblue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Gold; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Cyan; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4 Vesta</a> </span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:lime; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">1 Ceres</a></span></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_trajectory_as_of_September_2009.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="686" data-file-width="771" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Dawn_trajectory_as_of_September_2009.png/200px-Dawn_trajectory_as_of_September_2009.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Dawn_trajectory_as_of_September_2009.png/300px-Dawn_trajectory_as_of_September_2009.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Dawn_trajectory_as_of_September_2009.png/400px-Dawn_trajectory_as_of_September_2009.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_trajectory_as_of_September_2009.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Dawn</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> approximate flight trajectory</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i>Dawn</i> mission\'s goal was to characterize the conditions and processes of the Solar System\'s earliest eon by investigating in detail two of the largest protoplanets remaining intact since their formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180907_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180907-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Objectives_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Objectives-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the mission has finished, the data analyses and interpretations will continue for many years. The primary question that the mission addresses is the role of size and water in determining the evolution of the planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Objectives_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Objectives-38">[38]</a></sup> Ceres and Vesta are highly suitable bodies with which to address this question, as they are two of the most massive of the protoplanets. Ceres is geologically very primitive and icy, while Vesta is evolved and rocky. Their contrasting characteristics are thought to have resulted from them forming in two different regions of the early Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Objectives_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Objectives-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are three principal scientific drivers for the mission. First, the <i>Dawn</i> mission can capture the earliest moments in the origin of the Solar System, granting an insight into the conditions under which these objects formed. Second, <i>Dawn</i> determines the nature of the building blocks from which the terrestrial planets formed, improving scientific understanding of this formation. Finally, it contrasts the formation and evolution of two small planets that followed very different evolutionary paths, allowing scientists to determine what factors control that evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Objectives_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-Objectives-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifications">Specifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Specifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_at_Launch_Pad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Dawn_at_Launch_Pad.jpg/200px-Dawn_at_Launch_Pad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Dawn_at_Launch_Pad.jpg/300px-Dawn_at_Launch_Pad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Dawn_at_Launch_Pad.jpg/400px-Dawn_at_Launch_Pad.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_at_Launch_Pad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Dawn</i> prior to encapsulation at its launch pad on July 1, 2007</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dimensions">Dimensions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Dimensions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>With its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_array" title="Solar array">solar array</a> in the retracted launch position, the <i>Dawn</i> spacecraft is 2.36 meters (7.7&#160;ft) wide. With its solar arrays fully extended, <i>Dawn</i> is 19.7&#160;m (65&#160;ft) wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The solar arrays have a total area of 36.4&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (392&#160;sq&#160;ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The main antenna is five feet in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siliconvalleyastrolecture_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-siliconvalleyastrolecture-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propulsion_system">Propulsion system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Propulsion system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>Dawn</i> spacecraft was propelled by three <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a> <a href="/wiki/Ion_thruster" title="Ion thruster">ion thrusters</a> derived from <a href="/wiki/NASA_Solar_Technology_Application_Readiness" title="NASA Solar Technology Application Readiness">NSTAR</a> technology used by the <i>Deep Space 1</i> spacecraft,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> using one at a time. They have a <a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">specific impulse</a> of 3,100 s and produce a <a href="/wiki/Thrust" title="Thrust">thrust</a> of 90&#160;mN.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The whole spacecraft, including the ion propulsion thrusters, was powered by a 10&#160;kW (at 1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multijunction_photovoltaic_cell" title="Multijunction photovoltaic cell">triple-junction</a> <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">gallium arsenide</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_module" title="Photovoltaic module">photovoltaic</a> solar array manufactured by Dutch Space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> <i>Dawn</i> was allocated 247&#160;kg (545&#160;lb) of xenon for its Vesta approach, and carried another 112&#160;kg (247&#160;lb) to reach Ceres,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GarnerEtAl_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-GarnerEtAl-45">[45]</a></sup> out of a total capacity of 425&#160;kg (937&#160;lb) of on-board <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> With the propellant it carried, <i>Dawn</i> was able to perform a <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">velocity change</a> of approximately 11&#160;km/s over the course of its mission, far more than any previous spacecraft achieved with onboard propellant after separation from its launch rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GarnerEtAl_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-GarnerEtAl-45">[45]</a></sup> However, the thrust was very gentle; it took four days at full throttle to accelerate <i>Dawn</i> from zero to 60 mph (96 km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siliconvalleyastrolecture_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-siliconvalleyastrolecture-7">[7]</a></sup>\n<i>Dawn</i> is NASA\'s first purely exploratory mission to use ion propulsion engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The spacecraft also has twelve 0.9 N <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> thrusters for <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude control</a> (orientation), which were also used to assist in orbital insertion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Dawn spacecraft was able to achieve a record-breaking level of propulsion from its ion engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> NASA noted three specific areas of excellence:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>First to orbit two different astronomical bodies (not including Earth).</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Solar_electric_propulsion" title="Solar electric propulsion">Solar-electric propulsion</a> record, including a velocity change in space of 25,700 mph (11.49 km/second). This is 2.7x the velocity change by solar-electric ion drive than the past record.</li>\n<li>Achieved 5.9 years of ion engine runtime by September 7, 2018. This amount of runtime equates to 54% of Dawn\'s time in outer space.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outreach_microchip">Outreach microchip</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Outreach microchip">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Dawn</i> carries a memory <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">chip</a> bearing the names of more than 360,000 space enthusiasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The names were submitted online as part of a public outreach effort between September 2005 and November 4, 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The microchip, which is two centimetres in diameter, was installed on May 17, 2007, above the spacecraft\'s forward ion thruster, underneath its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-gain_antenna" title="High-gain antenna">high-gain antenna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> More than one microchip was made, with a back-up copy put on display at the 2007 Open House event at the <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a> in Pasadena, California.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Payload">Payload</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Payload">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19559-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-OpNav8-image1-20150516-crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/PIA19559-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-OpNav8-image1-20150516-crop.jpg/200px-PIA19559-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-OpNav8-image1-20150516-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/PIA19559-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-OpNav8-image1-20150516-crop.jpg/300px-PIA19559-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-OpNav8-image1-20150516-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/PIA19559-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-OpNav8-image1-20150516-crop.jpg/400px-PIA19559-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-OpNav8-image1-20150516-crop.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19559-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-OpNav8-image1-20150516-crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Framing camera view of the <a href="/wiki/Bright_spots_on_Ceres" title="Bright spots on Ceres">Ceres bright spots</a></div></div></div>\n<p>NASA\'s Jet Propulsion Laboratory provided overall planning and management of the mission, the flight system and scientific payload development, and provided the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_propulsion_system" title="Ion propulsion system">ion propulsion system</a>. Orbital Sciences Corporation provided the spacecraft, which constituted the company\'s first interplanetary mission. The <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Institute_for_Solar_System_Research" title="Max Planck Institute for Solar System Research">Max Planck Institute for Solar System Research</a> and the <a href="/wiki/German_Aerospace_Center" title="German Aerospace Center">German Aerospace Center</a> (DLR) provided the framing cameras, the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Space_Agency" title="Italian Space Agency">Italian Space Agency</a> provided the mapping <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectrometer</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a> provided the <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> spectrometer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman_2006_605&#8211;616_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman_2006_605&#8211;616-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Framing camera (FC)</b>&#160;&#8212; The framing camera uses 20&#160;mm aperture, f/7.9 refractive optical system with a focal length of 150&#160;mm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawn_Spacecraft_and_Instruments_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn_Spacecraft_and_Instruments-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sierks_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-sierks-55">[55]</a></sup> A frame-transfer <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">charge-coupled device</a> (CCD), a Thomson TH7888A,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sierks_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-sierks-55">[55]</a></sup> at the focal plane has 1024 &#215; 1024 sensitive 93-&#956;rad pixels, yielding a 5.5&#176; x 5.5&#176; field of view. An 8-position filter wheel permits <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panchromatic" title="Panchromatic">panchromatic</a> (clear filter) and spectrally selective imaging (7 narrow band filters). The broadest filter allows imaging at wavelengths ranging from 400 to 1050&#160;nm. In addition, the framing camera acquired images for optical navigation while in the vicinities of Vesta and Ceres. The FC computer is a custom <a href="/wiki/Radiation_hardening" title="Radiation hardening">radiation-hardened</a> <a href="/wiki/Xilinx" title="Xilinx">Xilinx</a> system with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LEON2" title="LEON2">LEON2</a> core and 8 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GiB" title="GiB">GiB</a> of memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sierks_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-sierks-55">[55]</a></sup> The camera offered  resolutions of 17 m/pixel for Vesta and 66 m/pixel for Ceres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sierks_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-sierks-55">[55]</a></sup> Because the framing camera was vital for both science and navigation, the payload had two identical and physically separate cameras (FC1 &amp; FC2) for redundancy, each with its own optics, electronics, and structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman_2006_605&#8211;616_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman_2006_605&#8211;616-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Visible and <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectrometer</a> (VIR)</b>&#160;&#8212; This instrument is a modification of the visible and infrared thermal-imaging spectrometer used on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rosetta_spacecraft" title="Rosetta spacecraft"><i>Rosetta</i></a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Venus_Express" title="Venus Express">Venus Express</a></i> spacecraft. It also draws its heritage from the <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> orbiter <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens"><i>Cassini</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s</a> visible and infrared mapping spectrometer. The spectrometer\'s VIR spectral frames are 256 (spatial) &#215; 432 (spectral), and the slit length is 64 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angular_mil" title="Angular mil">mrad</a>. The mapping spectrometer incorporates two channels, both fed by a single grating. A CCD yields frames from 0.25 to 1.0 &#956;m, while an array of HgCdTe photodiodes cooled to about 70K spans the spectrum from 0.95 to 5.0 &#956;m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman_2006_605&#8211;616_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman_2006_605&#8211;616-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Gamma Ray and Neutron Detector (GRaND)</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>&#160;&#8212; This instrument is based on similar instruments flown on the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Prospector" title="Lunar Prospector">Lunar Prospector</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars_Odyssey" title="Mars Odyssey">Mars Odyssey</a> space missions. This instrument includes 21 sensors with a very wide field of view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawn_Spacecraft_and_Instruments_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn_Spacecraft_and_Instruments-54">[54]</a></sup> It was be used to measure the abundances of the major rock-forming elements (oxygen, magnesium, aluminium, silicon, calcium, titanium, and iron) on Vesta and Ceres, as well as potassium, thorium, uranium, and water (inferred from hydrogen content).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometer</a> and laser <a href="/wiki/Altimeter" title="Altimeter">altimeter</a> were considered for the mission, but were not ultimately flown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_summary">Mission summary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mission summary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_preparations">Launch preparations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Launch preparations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On April 10, 2007, the spacecraft arrived at the Astrotech Space Operations subsidiary of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacehab" title="Spacehab">SPACEHAB, Inc.</a> in <a href="/wiki/Titusville,_Florida" title="Titusville, Florida">Titusville, Florida</a>, where it was prepared for launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The launch was originally scheduled for June 20, but was delayed until June 30 due to delays with part deliveries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> A broken crane at the launch pad, used to raise the <a href="/wiki/Solid_rocket_booster" title="Solid rocket booster">solid rocket boosters</a>, further delayed the launch for a week, until July 7; prior to this, on June 15, the second stage was successfully hoisted into position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> A mishap at the Astrotech Space Operations facility, involving slight damage to one of the solar arrays, did not have an effect on the launch date; however, bad weather caused the launch to slip to July 8. Range tracking problems then delayed the launch to July 9, and then July 15. Launch planning was then suspended in order to avoid conflicts with the <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(spacecraft)" title="Phoenix (spacecraft)"><i>Phoenix</i> mission</a> to Mars, which was successfully launched on August 4.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">Launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_ignition.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2008" decoding="async" height="329" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Dawn_ignition.jpg/220px-Dawn_ignition.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Dawn_ignition.jpg/330px-Dawn_ignition.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Dawn_ignition.jpg/440px-Dawn_ignition.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_ignition.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Dawn</i> launching on a <a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a> rocket from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Space_Launch_Complex_17" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Space Launch Complex 17">Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Space Launch Complex 17</a> on September 27, 2007</div></div></div>\n<p>The launch of <i>Dawn</i> was rescheduled for September 26, 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> then September 27, due to bad weather delaying fueling of the second stage, the same problem that delayed the July 7 launch attempt. The launch window extended from 07:20&#8211;07:49 EDT (11:20&#8211;11:49 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GMT" title="GMT">GMT</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> During the final built-in hold at T&#8722;4 minutes, a ship entered the exclusion area offshore, the strip of ocean where the rocket boosters were likely to fall after separation. After commanding the ship to leave the area, the launch was required to wait for the end of a collision avoidance window with the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <i>Dawn</i> finally launched from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Space_Launch_Complex_17" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Space Launch Complex 17">pad 17-B</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral Air Force Station</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta 7925-H</a> rocket<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> at 07:34 EDT,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> reaching escape velocity with the help of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-stabilized_satellite" title="Spin-stabilized satellite">spin-stabilized</a> solid-fueled third stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Thereafter, <i>Dawn\'s</i> ion thrusters took over.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Transit_.28Earth_to_Vesta.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Transit_(Earth_to_Vesta)">Transit (Earth to Vesta)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Transit (Earth to Vesta)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>After initial testing, during which the ion thrusters accumulated more than 11 days 14 hours of thrust, <i>Dawn</i> began long-term cruise propulsion on December 17, 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> On October 31, 2008, <i>Dawn</i> completed its first thrusting phase to send it on to Mars for a <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> flyby in February 2009. During this first interplanetary cruise phase, <i>Dawn</i> spent 270 days, or 85% of this phase, using its thrusters. It expended less than 72 kilograms of xenon propellant for a total change in velocity of 1.81&#160;km/s. On November 20, 2008, <i>Dawn</i> performed its first <a href="/wiki/Trajectory" title="Trajectory">trajectory</a> correction maneuver (TCM1), firing its number 1 thruster for 2 hours, 11 minutes.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="516" data-file-width="516" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Mars_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg/220px-Mars_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Mars_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg/330px-Mars_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Mars_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg/440px-Mars_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greyscale NIR image of Mars (northwest <a href="/wiki/Tempe_Terra" title="Tempe Terra">Tempe Terra</a>), taken by <i>Dawn</i> during its 2009 flyby</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Dawn</i> made its closest approach (549&#160;km) to <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> on February 17, 2009 during a successful gravity assist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> This flyby slowed Mars\' orbital velocity by about 2.5&#160;cm (1&#160;in) per 180 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siliconvalleyastrolecture_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-siliconvalleyastrolecture-7">[7]</a></sup> On this day, the spacecraft placed itself in <a href="/wiki/Safe_mode_(spacecraft)" title="Safe mode (spacecraft)">safe mode</a>, resulting in some data acquisition loss. The spacecraft was reported to be back in full operation two days later, with no impact on the subsequent mission identified. The <a href="/wiki/Root_cause" title="Root cause">root cause</a> of the event was reported to be a software programming error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jplstatus_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-jplstatus-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To cruise from Earth to its targets, <i>Dawn</i> traveled in an elongated outward spiral trajectory. The actual Vesta chronology and estimated<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (June 2017)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> Ceres chronology are as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSpaceTimeline_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-GSpaceTimeline-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>September 27, 2007: launch</li>\n<li>February 17, 2009: Mars <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a></li>\n<li>July 16, 2011: Vesta arrival and capture</li>\n<li>August 11&#8211;31, 2011: Vesta survey orbit</li>\n<li>September 29, 2011 &#8211; November 2, 2011: Vesta first high altitude orbit</li>\n<li>December 12, 2011 &#8211; May 1, 2012: Vesta low altitude orbit</li>\n<li>June 15, 2012 &#8211; July 25, 2012: Vesta second high altitude orbit</li>\n<li>September 5, 2012: Vesta departure</li>\n<li>March 6, 2015: Ceres arrival</li>\n<li>June 30, 2016: End of primary Ceres operations</li>\n<li>July 1, 2016: Beginning of Ceres extended mission<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfnow20160702_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfnow20160702-85">[85]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vesta_approach">Vesta approach</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Vesta approach">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As <i>Dawn</i> approached Vesta, the Framing Camera instrument took progressively higher-resolution images, which were published online and at news conferences by <a class="external text" href="http://dawn.jpl.nasa.gov/multimedia/vesta_dawn_gallery.asp" rel="nofollow">NASA</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://www.dawn.mps.mpg.de/index.php?id=10" rel="nofollow">MPI</a>.\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vesta_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="289" data-file-width="289" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Vesta_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg/120px-Vesta_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Vesta_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg/180px-Vesta_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Vesta_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg/240px-Vesta_image_by_Dawn_probe.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>June 14, 2011<br/>265,000&#160;km (165,000&#160;mi)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div (94,000&#160;mi)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:24.5px auto;"><a 2011<br/>100,000&#160;km (62,000&#160;mi)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dawn-image-070911.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="120" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Dawn-image-070911.jpg/240px-Dawn-image-070911.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>July 9, 2011<br/>41,000&#160;km (25,000&#160;mi)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<p>On May 3, 2011, <i>Dawn</i> acquired its first targeting image, 1,200,000&#160;km from Vesta, and began its approach phase to the asteroid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> On June 12, <i>Dawn\'s</i> speed relative to Vesta was slowed in preparation for its orbital insertion 34 days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Dawn</i> was scheduled to be inserted into orbit at 05:00 UTC on July 16 after a period of thrusting with its ion engines. Because its antenna was pointed away from the Earth during thrusting, scientists were not able to immediately confirm whether or not <i>Dawn</i> successfully made the maneuver. The spacecraft would then reorient itself, and was scheduled to check in at 06:30 UTC on July 17.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> NASA later confirmed that it received telemetry from <i>Dawn</i> indicating that the spacecraft successfully entered orbit around Vesta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The exact time of insertion could not be confirmed, since it depended on Vesta\'s mass distribution, which was not precisely known and at that time had only been estimated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vesta_orbit">Vesta orbit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Vesta orbit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>After being captured by Vesta\'s gravity and entering its orbit on July 16, 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencemag.org_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencemag.org-92">[92]</a></sup> <i>Dawn</i> moved to a lower, closer orbit by running its xenon-ion engine using solar power. On August 2, it paused its spiralling approach to enter a 69-hour survey orbit at an altitude of 2,750&#160;km (1,710&#160;mi). It assumed a 12.3-hour high-altitude mapping orbit at 680&#160;km (420&#160;mi) on September 27, and finally entered a 4.3-hour low-altitude mapping orbit at 210&#160;km (130&#160;mi) on December 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-status2011_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-status2011-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory_around_4_Vesta.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="300" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory_around_4_Vesta.gif 1.5x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" 2011<br/>16,000&#160;km (9,900&#160;mi)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div 2011<br/>10,500&#160;km (6,500&#160;mi)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div (3,200&#160;mi)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a (3,200&#160;mi)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<p>In May 2012, the <i>Dawn</i> team published preliminary results of their study of Vesta, including estimates of the size of Vesta\'s metal-rich core, which is theorized to be 220&#160;km (140&#160;mi) across. The scientists stated that they think that Vesta is the "last of its kind" &#8211; the only remaining example of the large planetoids that came together to form the rocky planets during the formation of the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencemag.org_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciencemag.org-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> In October 2012, further <i>Dawn</i> results were published, on the origin of anomalous dark spots and streaks on Vesta\'s surface, which were likely deposited by ancient asteroid impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> In December 2012, it was reported that <i>Dawn</i> had observed gullies on the surface of Vesta that were interpreted to have been eroded by transiently flowing liquid water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> More details about the <i>Dawn</i> mission\'s scientific discoveries at Vesta are included on the <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a> page.\n</p><p><i>Dawn</i> was originally scheduled to depart Vesta and begin its two and a half year journey to Ceres on August 26, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aug262012_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aug262012-9">[9]</a></sup> However, a problem with one of the spacecraft\'s <a href="/wiki/Reaction_wheel" title="Reaction wheel">reaction wheels</a> forced <i>Dawn</i> to delay its departure from Vesta\'s gravity until September 5, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5SepDepart_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-5SepDepart-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewSci2012_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewSci2012-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DawnDepartedVesta_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-DawnDepartedVesta-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yahoo_Dawn_Departs_Vesta_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yahoo_Dawn_Departs_Vesta-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg/600px-PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg/900px-PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg/1200px-PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg 2x" width="600"/></a>\n<center>The most ancient and heavily cratered regions are brown; areas modified by the <a href="/wiki/Veneneia" title="Veneneia">Veneneia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rheasilvia" title="Rheasilvia">Rheasilvia</a> impacts are purple (the Saturnalia Fossae Formation, in the north)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scully2014_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scully2014-108">[108]</a></sup> and light cyan (the Divalia Fossae Formation, equatorial),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2014_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2014-107">[107]</a></sup> respectively; the Rheasilvia impact basin interior (in the south) is dark blue, and neighboring areas of Rheasilvia ejecta (including an area within Veneneia) are light purple-blue;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch&#228;fer2014_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sch&#228;fer2014-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kneissl2014_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kneissl2014-110">[110]</a></sup> areas modified by more recent impacts or mass wasting are yellow/orange or green, respectively.</center>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span id="Transit_.28Vesta_to_Ceres.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Transit_(Vesta_to_Ceres)">Transit (Vesta to Ceres)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Transit (Vesta to Ceres)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable floatright">\n<caption>Imaging dates (2014&#8211;2015) and resolution<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Date</th>\n<th>distance <br/>(km)</th>\n<th>diameter <br/>(px)</th>\n<th>resolution <br/>(km/px)</th>\n<th>portion of disk<br/>illuminated\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>December 1</td>\n<td>1,200,000</td>\n<td>9</td>\n<td>112</td>\n<td>94%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>January 13</td>\n<td>383,000</td>\n<td>27</td>\n<td>36</td>\n<td>95%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>January 25</td>\n<td>237,000</td>\n<td>43</td>\n<td>22</td>\n<td>96%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>February 3</td>\n<td>146,000</td>\n<td>70</td>\n<td>14</td>\n<td>97%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>February 12</td>\n<td>83,000</td>\n<td>122</td>\n<td>7.8</td>\n<td>98%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>February 19</td>\n<td>46,000</td>\n<td>222</td>\n<td>4.3</td>\n<td>87%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>February 25</td>\n<td>40,000</td>\n<td>255</td>\n<td>3.7</td>\n<td>44%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>March 1</td>\n<td>49,000</td>\n<td>207</td>\n<td>4.6</td>\n<td>23%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>April 10</td>\n<td>33,000</td>\n<td>306</td>\n<td>3.1</td>\n<td>17%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>April 15</td>\n<td>22,000</td>\n<td>453</td>\n<td>2.1</td>\n<td>49%\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>During its time in orbit around Vesta, the probe experienced several failures of its reaction wheels. Investigators planned to modify their activities upon arrival at Ceres for close range geographical survey mapping. The <i>Dawn</i> team stated that they would orient the probe using a "hybrid" mode utilizing both reaction wheels and ion thrusters. Engineers determined that this hybrid mode would conserve fuel. On November 13, 2013, during the transit, in a test preparation, <i>Dawn</i> engineers completed a 27-hour-long series of exercises of said hybrid mode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On September 11, 2014, <i>Dawn</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> ion thruster unexpectedly ceased firing and the probe began operating in a triggered safe mode. To avoid a lapse in propulsion, the mission team hastily exchanged the active ion engine and electrical controller with another. The team stated that they had a plan in place to revive this disabled component later in 2014. The controller in the ion propulsion system may have been damaged by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-energy_particle" title="High-energy particle">high-energy particle</a>. Upon exiting the safe mode on September 15, 2014, the probe\'s ion thruster resumed normal operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SafeMode2014_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-SafeMode2014-113">[113]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Furthermore, the <i>Dawn</i> investigators also found that, after the propulsion issue, <i>Dawn</i> could not aim its main communications antenna towards Earth. Another antenna of weaker capacity was instead temporarily retasked. To correct the problem, the probe\'s computer was reset and the aiming mechanism of the main antenna was restored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SafeMode2014_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-SafeMode2014-113">[113]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ceres_approach">Ceres approach</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Ceres approach">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Dawn</i> began photographing an extended disk of Ceres on December 1, 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dawnjournal1214_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-dawnjournal1214-114">[114]</a></sup> with images of partial rotations on January 13 and 25, 2015 released as animations.\nImages taken from <i>Dawn</i> of Ceres after January 26, 2015, exceeded the resolution of comparable images from the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div align="center"><b>Progression of images of Ceres taken by <i>Dawn</i> between January and March 2015</b></div>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" <i>Dawn</i> made fewer camera observations of Ceres during its approach phase than it did during its Vesta approach. Camera observations required turning the spacecraft, which consumed precious hydrazine fuel. Seven optical navigation photo sessions (OpNav 1&#8211;7, on January 13 and 25, February 3 and 25, March 1, and April 10 and 15) and two full rotation observation sessions (RC1&#8211;2, on February 12 and 19) were planned<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (December 2015)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> before full observation begins with orbital capture. The gap in March and early April was due to a period when Ceres appears too close to the Sun from <i>Dawn</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> vantage point to take pictures safely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ceres_orbit">Ceres orbit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dawn_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Ceres orbit">edit</a><span 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>December 10, 2015<br/><b>4th Map Orbit - LAMO</b><br/>385&#160;km (240&#160;mi)<br/><span style="font-size:85%;">(<a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Dawn_Ceres_LAMO_phase" title="commons:Category:Dawn Ceres LAMO phase">view on commons</a>)</span>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA21248_-_Dawn_XMO2_Image_28.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="120"
